<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [2]
some appearances were to groups,</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [3]
they were repeated appearances,</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [4]
unbelievers were involved, and</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> [5]
his own disciples did not expect him to rise.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) Alternative
explanations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (a) Hallucination</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1] He
appeared to more than one at once. (Note especially </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> Matthew
<b>28</b>:16-20; 1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:6.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [2] They
did not expect him to resurrect.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] They
not only saw but touched and heard him.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [4] More
than one person saw him at the same time.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (b) Mistaken
identity</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1] All
the detailed experiences the Twelve had with him during his ministry and after
his resurrection make mistaken identity incredible. There were some occasions
when he was not recognized immediately: Paul on the road to Damascus (Acts <b>9</b>,
<i>etc</i>.), the two on the way to Emmaus (Luke <b>24</b>), Peter and the others
by the Sea of Galilee (John <b>21</b>), Mary Magdalene (John <b>20</b>). In
some appearances perhaps the resurrection altered the looks of the man by
making him look younger or different from his previous appearance as a
crucified and dying man. This lack of immediate recognition is sufficiently
explained by their utter lack of expecting to see him.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   [2]  The
nail scars in his hands and feet along with the spear thrust in his side were
decisive marks that resulted from his manner of execution. As he had previously
to the other ten disciples, Jesus offered these marks to Thomas, who had
previously entertained doubt. Thomass response is well-known (John<b> 20</b>:20,
25, 27-28; Luke <b>24</b>:39-40). The two recognized him in Emmaus during the
breaking of bread. Maybe they saw the scars that showed in the light as he
passed the food around or perhaps in his manner of taking the loaf and blessing
it, which was his custom.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] The
disciples watched him do things that people do not dothings that Jesus himself
had not done during his ministry. He <u>materialized</u> to them inside closed
doors (John<b> 20</b>:19, 26). He <u>dematerialized</u> at Emmaus (Luke <b>24</b>:31).
He <u>ascended</u> to heaven bodily in front of them outside Jerusalem (Mark <b>16</b>:19;
Luke <b>24</b>:51; Acts <b>1</b>:9-11).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   [4] The
encounters were near approachesin a small room, close enough to touch, around
a table, Mary clinging to him (John<b> 20</b>:17).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [5] Several
appearances were during the daytime. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (c) Epileptic
seizure in the case of Paul</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   [1] Others
saw the light and heard the voice.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   [2] He
was qualified for apostleship by this experience, so it </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>was objective (Acts<b> 22</b>:14; <b>26</b>:16; 1
Corinthians<b> 9</b>:1).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] He
was blinded by the bright light (Acts<b> 22</b>:11).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (d) An
apparition</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  He ate
and drank with them (Acts<b> 10</b>:41; Luke<b> 24</b>:42-43; John </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>21</b>:1?).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (e) Liars</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1] A
person will not give his life for what he knows is false </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1
Corinthians <b>15</b>:30-32).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [2] The
apostles championed a high ethical standard.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] The
accounts have features not readily reconcilable, which tends to show that they
were not written with any sense of needing to harmonize apparent discrepancies.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c. Church</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The believableness of
the resurrection is not wholly dependent on the evidence for one unique event. That
event ties into other matters that anticipated it and issued from it. To a
significant extent, the acceptableness of Jesus Messiah depended on the Jews
proper understanding of Old Testament expectations about the Christ. This web
of reinforcing evidences increases exponentially the power of the data. A
different understanding predisposed most nationalistically minded Jews to
reject him on doctrinal grounds. Nevertheless, as Gamaliels presentation shows
in Acts <b>5</b>:34-40, the argument from the existence of the church tended to
short-circuit that doctrinal objection sufficiently to force a re-evaluation of
prophetic interpretation and current policy toward Jesus followers.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Arguing from the empty
tomb and from the appearances of the resurrected Messiah depends largely on the
reliability of New Testament documents. The argument from the existence of the
church, however, is more loosely tied to the records, because historical
details would not have to be accurate in every case for the argument to hold. Even
a cursory reading of the New Testament shows that the church staked its claim
on whether Jesus came forth alive from his tomb in Jerusalem (1 Corinthians<b>
15</b>:14; cp. 1 Thessalonians<b> 4</b>:14).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The disciples did not go
away to some other place to begin their proclamation, some place where people
would have had little possibility of corroborating their story. They proclaimed
their message right in the place where they could have been accused of stealing
the body or dealing with a misidentified tomb or not having an empty tomb.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A person cannot
determine the correctness of a viewpoint by the number of people who accept it.
People reject things for emotional reasons, for the practical advantage it
implies for them, and for any number of other such reasons.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A person-centered system
disintegrates at the death of the central person.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt'><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) The
resurrection of Jesus of Nazareth plausibly explains the </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> continuation of
his movement after his death (Acts <b>5</b>:34-40).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  When Jewish
authorities arrested the apostles and were minded to execute them for preaching
Jesus as Messiah, Gamaliel restrained the Sanhedrin by observing that this
movement was not following the pattern of other, false, Messianic movements. In
the cases of Judas and Theudas, their deaths led to the natural consequence: the
movements disintegrated. In the case of Jesus of Nazareth, his death not only
failed to stop the movement, but it gained impetus. His resurrection could
explain that unexpected pattern, and so the council heeded Gamaliels advice to
let them alone and to put them forth a little while.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  We must enter
the contemporary Jewish mindset to see the force of Gamaliels argument. Instead
of supposing that this doctor of the law used the occasion to score a point
against the Sadducees in the council (maybe secondary motivation all right), we
propose that his reasoning had enough cogency that it relaxed the persecution
of Christs disciples sufficiently for disciples to multiply in Jerusalem and a
great numbers of priests to believe (Acts<b> 6</b>:7). Only after an indeterminate
time, Gamaliels disciple, Saul of Tarsus, brought in a wave of persecution
beginning with Stephens martyrdom that lasted until he himself was converted
in Damascus.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) The
first element is that <u>Messiah is eternal</u>. During the ministry of Jesus
that idea surfaced as one stumbling block when Jesus publicly intimated that he
would die by crucifixion (John<b> 12</b>:34; Matthew<b> 26</b>:35). From the Law
the crowd knew that Messiah was to abide forever. Typical passages include
Psalm <b>110</b>:4; Isaiah <b>9</b>:7; Ezekiel <b>37</b>:25; and Daniel <b>7</b>:14.
Since there can be only one universal eternal kingdom (as per Psalm<b> 89</b>;
Daniel<b> 7</b>:14), any prophetic passage dealing with either a universal or
eternal kingdom was speaking of the Messiah and his kingdom. Especially under
the pressure of Roman domination, the Jews had fixed on that permanent liberation.
For a Messianic claimant to imply that he would die was doubly unthinkable: the
prophets had said he would abide forever, and the people needed him to deliver
them permanently from political oppression. Jews would not follow a Jewish
Messiah they knew was dead.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Not
only did the masses subscribe to this understanding, but the inner circle of
Jesus disciples assumed it. At Jesus disclosure that he would have to go to
Jerusalem, suffer, and die, Peter promptly took him aside and rebuked him, not
seeming to hear the further comments that death would not prevail against
establishing the kingdom because he would resurrect on the third day (Matthew <b>16</b>:13-28).
Before the crucifixion the disciples followed the expected pattern of fear
(John <b>20</b>:19) and dispersal that later turned to atypical boldness and
power.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  For
the Jewish leaders there was one practical consequence of the Messiahs
eternality: the very fact they could kill him meant he was not the Christ. The
incredible behavior of passersby, the soldiers, the rulers of the people, and
even those who died with him takes on at least a modicum of sensibleness with
this observation (Matthew <b>27</b>:29-44; Mark <b>15</b>:29-32; Luke<b> 23</b>:35-37).
Before his crucifixion the two Emmaus residents had hoped that it was he who
would redeem Israel (Luke <b>24</b>:21). Now that he was dead the chief
priests could confidently call him that deceiver (Matthew <b>27</b>:63). He
did not abide forever; therefore, he was not the Christ.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) The
second element in the picture is that <u>the Messianic kingdom is
person-centered</u>. By virtue of eternality, Messiahs kingdom accentuates the
person-centered character of the normal kingdom model; here the same person is
king forever. There exists no real parallel to the Messiah and his kingdom. A
person-centered system disintegrates when the personal center goes away. It is
this point that Gamaliel makes when he contrasts the present case with previous
ones that followed the expected pattern natural to Messianism.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1]  What
we affirm here differs from an <u>ideology-centered system</u>. Communism did
not die with the death of Karl Marx. The movement of a charismatic leader may disintegrate
with his death. A case in point is Adolf Hitler and his doctrine of the
supremacy of the Aryan race. The cause lost a forceful leader and for that
and other reasons fell apart, but it did not have to do so. An ideology does
not depend on a person for its existence, because someone else can step in to
carry it forward. If the ideology somehow proves to be false scientifically or
philosophically, the movement centered around it will dissolve. An
ideology-centered movement dies when its ideological center dies in the same
way that a person-centered one dies if its person dies. The Christ was not the
dead founder of a religious movement; he is the living Lord of an eternal
kingdom.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [2] What
is affirmed here also differs from <u>sins tendency to self-destruct</u>. Sin
does tend to self-destruct since sin is usually sin because it is out of keeping
with the nature of the thing or situation involved; consequently, when the
organism or the group conducts itself contrary to its nature or purpose, a
process of natural selection removes it from the competition. Sins
self-destruction may involve centuries, whereas the matter under consideration
in Gamaliels speech is a little while.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] Later,
during the preaching about the resurrection, those mens lives were endangered,
they were beaten, some were killed. Had their claims for Jesus resurrection
been false, they would surely not have died horrible deaths for a [a] known [b]
falsehood that was [c] contrary to their convictions as Jews.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [4] From
an ethical standpoint, we find it difficult to believe that so high a moral
standard as the believers advocated (and demonstrated in many ways) would allow
for perpetrating such a fraud.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> When
the rabbi affirmed <i>If this counsel comes from people it will be
overthrown,</i> he did not mean it as a general principle in history, we take
it, but as a principle applicable to the matter at handthe Messiah and his
kingdom. That Jesus of Nazareth had resurrected Gamaliel could in principle
accept as a Pharisee. Also, Jesus resurrection not only (1) explained the
peculiarity of this case, but it (2) provided a way to join the suffering and
glory motifs in Old Testament prophecy. It (3) tied in with Jesus prediction
of his resurrection and (4) harmonized with his miraculous ministry, especially
the raising of Lazarus (John<b> 11</b>:1-53), a particularly embarrassing sign
performed right under the rulers noses. The resurrection (5) accounted for
the disappearance of Jesus body and (6) made sense of the disciples
indomitable spirit. It (7) fit with continued miracles like the healing the
well-known impotent man at Gate Beautiful (Acts <b>3-4</b>). The cumulative
force of these considerations made Gamaliels caution a wise one.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Summary Analysis of Gamaliels Speech</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;
margin-bottom:0in;margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a)  <u>Political</u>.
<i>They came to nothing</i> means that (1) the Romans will take care of this
as they did in the case of Theudas and Judas; we need not get involved. (2) <i>Minded
to execute them</i> relates to the fact that Rome had withdrawn Jewish right
to exercise capital punishment (except where the temple precincts were
desecrated). Gamaliels suggestion kept them from ill-advised action that could
bring worse trouble on the religious leaders than Jesus Messianic movement would
bring.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b)  <u>Pharisaical</u>.
He is willing to use this occasion to score a point </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> against
the Sadducees.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (c)  <u>Theological</u>.
The resurrection that the disciples were proclaiming would explain the
different direction this movement has taken after its leaders death (<i>vs</i>.
<i>came to nothing</i>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) People will not
give their lives in torture for what they know is false when it is contrary to
their convictions (1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:30-32). Jesus resurrection explains
the willingness of his disciples to give their lives for their commitment to
him as Messiah. Many people would not give their lives for what they know is
true, much less for what they know is false.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> The point here follows
what has been said before about the collapse of a person-centered system after
the death of the central person. There may be conceivable situations in which people
will give their lives for what they know is false if it might mean protecting loved
ones or if they believe they will go down in history as honored persons. In
general, though, Satans words ring true, <i>Everything mortals have they will
give for their lives</i> (Job <b>2</b>:4). Giving ones life is a persons
last measure of devotion. Going down in history as an honored person does not
really apply to the apostles because it was regarding someone else that they suffered
martyrdom. Furthermore, as we said repeatedly before, they represented an ethic
that opposed the approach to living that they would have employed to pull off
such deception. More about the moral considerations later; as for now, Pauls
argument in 1 Corinthians <b>15</b> focuses on suffering as a <i>non sequitur</i>
if disciples knew Jesus as dead.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Paul begins his evidence
with a catalog of appearances by the resurrected Christ, naming last his own
experience near Damascus. He places that evidence in opposition to some
heretical teaching in Corinth about resurrection. His presentation he weaves
into a line of thought as follows:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> If there is no
resurrection, Christ has not risen (<b>15</b>:13).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> If Christ has
not risen, we are false witnesses (<b>15</b>:15).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> If we are false
witnesses, why do we suffer (<b>15</b>:19-34)?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Another direction for
the reasoning here comes in <b>15</b>:14: the apostolic preaching is
meaningless and the Corinthians faith in it is likewise meaningless. That
thrust is set aside in the interest of showing that he did rise; so the kerygma
is not meaningless.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a)  One
observation about Pauls argument is that <u>the final basis is behavioral, not
logical</u>. Christianity is appropriately defended by historical rather than philosophical
arguments because it has the character of event, not idea.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (b) Secondly,
<u>Paul does not entertain the possibility that the apostles were deceived</u>.
He calls himself a <u>false</u> witness as a reflection of the utter realism of
his own witness of the resurrected Christ.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) The moral
commitments of the Christian system militate against the </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> apostles lying
to propagate the system.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d.  Old Testament prediction
of the (death-)resurrection of Messiah</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  During the ministry of
Jesus, he predicted not only that he would die, but that he would resurrect on
the third day: John<b> 2</b>:20-22; Matthew <b>16</b>:21-28 (= Mark <b>8</b>:31-<b>9</b>:1;
Luke <b>9</b>:22-27); <b>27</b>:63;<b> 28</b>:1-8 (Luke <b>24</b>:5-8); Luke <b>24</b>:44-48.
Beyond his own predictions is the New Testament claim in the mouth of Jesus and
his disciples that the Old Testament prophesied Messiahs resurrection. The
unexplained claim appears in Luke <b>24</b>:25-27, 32; <b>24</b>:44-48; John<b>
20</b>:9; Acts <b>26</b>:22-23, and 1 Corinthians<b> 15</b>:4. Other passages
specify the prophecy.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) <u>Psalm <b>16</b>:8-11</u>.
According to Acts <b>2</b>:25-31 and<b> 13</b>:34-37 the reference in Psalm<b>
16</b> is to Messiahs resurrection when it declares, <i>Neither will you give
your Holy One to see corruption.</i> Peter argues that the text could not refer
to David because his flesh saw corruption and his hearers could still visit his
tomb. Psalm <b>16</b>:10 seems to convey the following conception: <i>You will
not leave my</i> [Davids] <i>soul/life in Sheol/Hades/the grave nor allow your
holy one to see corruption.</i> Davids life/soul would rest in hope (<b>16</b>:9)
and not be left in the grave, because the son of David would resurrect; the
parallelism of <b>16</b>:10 suggests a relationship between the two points. In
Acts <b>2</b>:31 Peter speaks of both halves of Psalm <b>16</b>:10 as referring
to Messiah, perhaps not because the first directly relates to him, but because
the Messiahs not being left to corruption implies Davids not being left in
the grave and so the latter idea may in scriptural wording apply to him as
well.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) <u>Isaiah <b>52</b>:13-<b>53</b>:12</u>.
Known as the Suffering Servant poem, Isaiah <b>52</b>:13-<b>53</b>:12 has been
understood by the Christian world as a Messianic reference, although that view
may not have prevailed in first-century Judaism. No New Testament writer uses
this poem as prophesying the Messiahs resurrection, though it may have been so
used by Philip in unrecorded parts of the presentation to the Ethiopian eunuch
(Acts <b>8</b>:26-36). Isaiah <b>53</b>:10b says, <i>When you make his
soul/life an offering for sin, he will see his seedhe will prolong his days
and the pleasure of Yahveh will prosper in his hand.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) <u>Psalm<b> 2</b>:7-9</u>.
In Acts <b>13</b>:32-33 (cp. Matthew <b>22</b>:41-45; Hebrews <b>1</b>:5; <b>5</b>:5).
Paul applies the prediction to the resurrection probably as implied by the
enthronement idea in light of the gospel events that have already transpired.
Messiahs resurrection does not seem to be directly and exegetically
determinable from this Old Testament text itself.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.25in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (4) In Acts <b>13</b>:34
Paul clusters a reference to Isaiah<b> 55</b>:3 with Psalms<b> 2</b> and <b>16</b>
probably because of the eternal covenant made with everyone that thirsts (<b>55</b>:1)
among the peoples</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:red'> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>(<b>55</b>:4-5) who receive the witness of David. By
taking David as a dynastic designation, Paul must understand the prince and
leader to mean the Messiah, since David was long dead by the time of Isaiah. The
eternality of his leadership may imply the resurrection since humanness
includes physical death. The point, however, would not be required by this
text, which would be problematic only to a pre-Christian reader.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Principally two passages
predict the Messiahs resurrection: Psalm <b>16</b>:8-10 and Isaiah<b> 52</b>:13-<b>53</b>:12.
The event derives exegetically from the texts whereas in others it fits with
the texts.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.  Revealed meaning</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The death-resurrection of
Jesus is one composite event with a meaning made clear <u>through</u> it and <u>in
connection with</u> it (cp. John <b>13</b>:19; <b>14</b>:29. That the
death-resurrection belongs together is clear from the event itself as well as
from Romans<b> 4</b>:25. Paul says that Jesus our Lord <i>was delivered up for
our transgressions and was raised for our justification.</i> The New Testament
states many things <u>in connection with</u> it, but the question remains as to
how much is clear <u>through</u> it. The meaning of the death of the man on the
middle cross would not be visibly different from the deaths of the men on
either side of him, but keeping the resurrection in mind broadens the base for
direct inference from the event itself as well as for what is made <u>clear by
proclamation</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.  Implications of the
death-resurrection</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) <u>The fact of
resurrection itself</u>. If Jesus of Nazareth arose from the dead, there is
such a thing as resurrection to immortality. When we connect this point with
the teaching of Jesus and his apostles, it generalizes to everyone.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) <u>Ethical
implications</u>. If there is resurrection to immortality, the way a person
lives must take into account more than life before the grave. It must not then
be limited to the dictates of the flesh (Colossians<b> 3</b>:1-4; Romans <b>6</b>:1-14;
1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:31-32; Philippians<b> 3</b>:17-21).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) <u>Validation of
Jesus previous teaching and claims</u>. No man can resurrect from the dead
except God is with him. God will not validate a person who is teaching,
claiming, or doing contrary to Gods will. He <i>was declared to be the Son of
God . . . by the resurrection</i> (Romans<b> 1</b>:4). Victory, not defeat,
is the verdict.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:4.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) <u>Interpretation
implications</u>. A new hermeneutical programmatic needs to be applied to Old
Testament expectations for the Messianic age.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5)  <u>Theological
implications</u>: Material existence is not an evil.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b.  Old Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Among the claims of
Jesus was that he was the long-expected Messiah. The resurrection having
validated those claims, Old Testament prophecies about the death-resurrection
become especially significant, because in connection with those prophecies the New
Testament states the significance of his death. The meaning of the
death-resurrection is seen to be <u>in</u> it (rather than read into it) by a pre-statement
of its meaning. God intended a certain significance into the death-resurrection
and vouchsafed it to his servants by prophecy. Jesus claimed to be Messiah; his
resurrection authenticated his claims; the Old Testament teaching on Messiahs
death indicates the meaning of the death-resurrection of Jesus.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The main observation about
the death of Jesus according to Old Testament prophecy is that the crucifixion
is analogous to a sacrifice for sin. It has atonement implications.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c.  New Testament reflection</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  In consequence of Old
Testament prophecy about the significance of Messiahs death, New Testament writers
reflect on that event, further elaborating the meaning it contains. For those
matters beyond what is directly obvious and what is predictively asserted, miracle
serves to confirm the apostolic preaching, and so the place of miracle in the
historical basis of Christian faith must receive attention.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3. Miraculous considerations</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.  Miracle and the meaning
of events</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1)  Prediction, by giving
access to the knowledge of a future event, </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> gives access to
its meaning as well.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (2) Other miracles,
by demonstrating the power to perform them, </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> demonstrates
their meaning as well.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>The following are the senses in which the events give
rise to the doctrine (meaning) </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;color:windowtext'>in
station two of the triangle:</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'> (a) God
intends the meaning into the events in contrast to observers reading it into
them; (b) we can deduce some meanings from the occurrence itself: since
resurrection has occurred, there is such a thing as resurrection, and so on;
(c) miracle confirms the claims of the miracle worker (doctrinal matters) (Mark
<b>2</b>:1-12; Romans<b> 1</b>:1-7).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b.  Miracle as authentication
of revelation</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Divine revelation always
involves supernatural involvement either as the medium of revelation itself or
as an accompanying factor that authenticates the revelation. As Mark<b> 2</b>:9-10
proves the claim that Jesus had the right on earth to forgive sins, John <b>11</b>:42
uses the resurrection of Lazarus to prove that God had sent Jesus. Miracle is
proof of truth appears in the following texts: Mark <b>2</b>:5-12 (= Matthew<b>
9</b>:2-8; Luke <b>5</b>:18-26); <b>16</b>:20; John <b>2</b>:11; <b>3</b>:2; <b>5</b>:36;
<b>9</b>:16b, 30-33;<b> 10</b>:25, 37-38; <b>11</b>:42; 1<b>2</b>:37; <b>14</b>:11
(<b>15</b>:24); <b>20</b>:30; Acts <b>10</b>:38; <b>15</b>:12; 1 Corinthians<b>
14</b>:22. Although miracle authenticates revelation, people do not necessarily
believe what is affirmed in connection with miracle. The following
considerations also belong in the picture.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c.  Miracle as authenticated
by previous revelation</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Several passages enjoin
the faithful to test miraculous events by previous revelation: Deuteronomy <b>13</b>:1-5;
<b>18</b>:20-22; Mark<b> 2</b>:5-10; <b>9</b>:39 (cp. 1 Corinthians <b>12</b>:3);
John <b>9</b>:16a; Galatians <b>1</b>:8-9; 1 John <b>4</b>:1-4.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Testing a miracle by
doctrine while claiming miracle as proof of doctrine is neither circular nor
self-contradictory. The reason both processes can be true is that (a) it is not
the same doctrine that is used to test the miracle as is proved by the miracle.
The point is that Gods revelation is consistent over time: John <b>16</b>:13-15
+ Jude 3; Galatians <b>1</b>:8-9; Deuteronomy <b>13</b>:1-5; 1 Peter <b>3</b>:18
and Hebrews <b>9</b>:26-28 (once for all time). Furthermore, (b) there is
always the possibility that the miraculous display is demonic, a paranormal
phenomenon, a falsehood-illusion-magic trick, a psychosomatic result, or sheer
chance. Something is wrong somewhere if a miracle accompanies a false
teaching. Either the miracle is not divine, a persons understanding of
previous revelation is wrong, or it is not in fact supernatural. Paramount here
is the unmistakable fact that a given phenomenon is indeed a divine miracle.
The pattern below outlines its sweep from Moses to Christ.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <img width=355 height=97
src="/cir-library/images/essays/part-i-overview-of-christian-apologetics/image005.png"></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1) The original
establishment: Moses</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) Stupendous
miracles: Egyptian plagues, crossing the Red Sea, manna, quail, pillar of smoke
and fire, manifestations at Sinai, healing from snakebite, earthquakes,
consumption by fire, clothes that did not wear out</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b)  Triumph
over the Egyptian bull-god Apis and the Egyptian </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> magicians</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (c)  Pronouncement
of false on all other alternatives (gods)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (d)  Prediction
that other prophets would arise and their further revelations would be consistent
with the normative prophet Moses (Deuteronomy <b>18</b>:15-22).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) The coming of
other prophets that reaffirmed the Mosaic economy and predicted its abrogation
nationally speaking (Jeremiah<b> 31</b>:31-34; cp. Hebrews <b>8-10</b>)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) The subsequent
establishment: Christ</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) Stupendous
miracles: resurrection, exorcising, and the like. There is some value to the
sheer number of miracles Jesus did (John<b> 7</b>:31).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) Resurrection
of the crucified Messiah</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (c) Abrogation
of the previous economy as per Jeremiah <b>31 </b>and the pronouncement of false
on all other lords (1 Corinthians <b>8</b>:5)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (d) Affirmation
of the finality of the new economy (Jude <b>3</b>)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Two starting points
apologetically and theologically in the Judaeo- Christian religion correspond
to the beginning of the two main successive economies. In both there was
stupendous display to dispel doubt and authenticate claims. At the same time,
there was success in confrontation with alternativesdemonic and otherwise. The
circularity between doctrine and miracle is avoided, because it is not the same
doctrine that tests miracle that was established by miracle. The original
doctrine was accompanied by stupendous display and triumph over demonic power. That
doctrine became a restrictive test on all subsequent doctrinal claims. If
subsequent claims were miraculously accompanied, they were still rejected
because they did not harmonize with previous doctrine. New doctrine is
authenticated by miracle that observers can distinguish from demonic miracle,
deception, or paranormal occurrences. In the modern setting, beyond such marks
we have the understanding that the revelation in Jesus is final (Jude 3), so
nothing will supersede the gospel as the gospel superseded the Law.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d. Miracle as challenged by
demonic power</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Saying above that an
apparent miracle could be demonic assumes (a) the existence of demonic power
and possession. Some biblical examples of demon possession are Matthew <b>4</b>:24;
<b>8</b>:16-17 (= Mark <b>1</b>:32-34; Luke <b>4</b>:40-41); <b>8</b>:28-34 (=
Mark <b>5</b>:1-20; Luke <b>8</b>:26-37); <b>9</b>:32-24; <b>12</b>:22-28 (Mark
<b>3</b>:22-30; Luke<b> 11</b>:14-20); <b>15</b>:21-28 (= Mark <b>7</b>:24-30).
Other references to demonic phenomena include Matthew <b>7</b>:22; <b>10</b>:8;
<b>11</b>:18; <b>17</b>:14-18 (= Luke <b>9</b>:37-42); Mark<b> 1</b>:39; <b>3</b>:15;<b>
6</b>:13; <b>9</b>:38 (= Luke <b>9</b>:49-50); <b>16</b>:9 (<b>8</b>:1-2), 17;
Luke<b> 4</b>:33-37; <b>7</b>:33; <b>9</b>:1; <b>10</b>:17; <b>13</b>:22; John <b>7</b>:20;
<b>8</b>:48-49; Acts <b>17</b>:18; <b>19</b>:12; 1 Corinthians <b>10</b>:20-22;
1 Timothy<b> 4</b>:1; James <b>2</b>:19; Revelation <b>9</b>:20.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  That an apparent miracle
could be demonic assumes also (b) that demonic and divine miracle can be
distinguished. They are at least distinguishable: Acts<b> 13</b>:4-12; <b>19</b>:13-
20; compare<b> 8</b>:1-24. In that connection note the following points.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) <u>The divine
triumphs over the demonic in confrontation</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) Moses
and the Egyptian magicians: Exodus <b>7-11</b></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) Paul and
Elymas the sorcerer: Acts <b>13</b>:6-20</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (c) Paul
and the Ephesian occult: Acts <b>19</b>:13-29</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (d) All
examples of exorcism: Matthew <b>12</b>:28-29, for example</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (e) The
demonic cannot overcome the believer, at least contrary to his will: Mark <b>5</b>:1-17
(= Matthew <b>8</b>:28-43; Luke<b> 8</b>:26-37); John<b> 6</b>:39; 10:28; <b>17</b>:12;
<b>18</b>:9</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (f) Joseph:
Genesis <b>40-41</b></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (g) Daniel
and the pagan wise men: Daniel<b> 5</b></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (h) Philip
and Simon the sorcerer: Acts<b> 8</b></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (i) 1 John<b>
4</b>:1-4</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) <u>Divine miracle
glorifies God</u> (Matthew <b>5</b>:16; <b>9</b>:8 = Mark <b>2</b>:12 = Luke<b>
5</b>:25-26; Matthew<b> 15</b>:31; Luke<b> 7</b>:16; <b>13</b>:13; <b>17</b>:15;
Acts <b>4</b>:21; <b>21</b>:17-20).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) The
demonic cannot act in Gods name: Mark<b> 9</b>:39 (Luke <b>9</b>:49-50); Acts <b>19</b>:13-16.
The use of Jesus name does not give automatic results. We may make allowance
here for a case like that described by Ben Alexander in <i>Exposing Satans
Power</i>, XIII:I, p. 3, where a person erroneously thinks he is doing miracles
by <u>Gods</u> power, when it is demonic instead. We might make allowance for people
erroneously thinking they are doing <u>miracles</u> by Gods power (Matthew <b>7</b>:22-23?).
Kathryn Kuhlman is a case according to William Nolens analysis in <i>Healing: Doctor
in Search of a Miracle</i>, pp. 72-102. We must also make allowance for a
sorcerers followers thinking it is divine miracle (Acts <b>8</b>:9-11).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) Divine
miracle does not aim to benefit the miracle worker.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1] There
are no biblical examples of miracles worked by a miracle worker <u>on himself</u>.
Note Pauls thorn in the flesh (2 Corinthians<b> 12</b>:7-9 = Galatians<b> 4</b>:13?)
and Jesus refusal to turn stones into bread during his temptation (Matthew <b>4</b>:3-4
= Luke <b>4</b>:3-5). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [2] There
are not many clear examples of miracles performed <u>on a fellow Christian</u>.
Note Trophimus in 2 Timothy <b>4</b>:20 that Paul was left behind sick instead
of healing him. An exception is the raising of Dorcas in Acts <b>9</b>:36-43.
During Jesus ministry he resurrected his disciple Lazarus and healed Peters
mother-in-law (Matthew <b>8</b>:14-17). Eutychus also may be an exception in
Acts <b>20</b>:9-12. Having some Christians not delivered from death for their
faith seems odd if others are having a shorter leg lengthened by an inch. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] There
is no example of a divine representative taking <u>money for his service</u>.
Contrast Acts <b>3</b>:6;<b> 8</b>:18-24 with Acts <b>16</b>:16-19ff.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [4] There
is no example of an approved use of miracles that draws attention to the
miracle worker. Note 1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:3-19, 28; Acts <b>3</b>:11-12ff;<b>
4</b>:9-10; Luke <b>10</b>:17- 20; John<b> 7</b>:18 in contrast to Acts <b>8</b>:9-11.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) <u>The demonic
cannot agree to given doctrines</u> (?): 1 John <b>4</b>:4. Although a common
observation, this idea is evidently not true in all cases (cp. Mark <b>1</b>:24;
note also 2 Thessalonians <b>2</b>:9; 1 Timothy<b> 4</b>:1-2; spirit used
here as in Acts <b>6</b>:10).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) <u>In divine
miracle the spirit is subject to the person</u> (1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:32)
while in demonic miracle the person is subject to the spirit (Luke <b>9</b>:37-43,
<i>e.g</i>.). The Spirit of God operates <u>through</u> peoples personality,
not <u>in place of</u> it. He uses peoples personality, not just their body.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5) <u>The demonic
does not have positive effect</u> as measured in the total situation (Matthew<b>
12</b>:25-26 = Mark <b>3</b>:23-26 = Luke <b>11</b>:17-18). The idea here does
not necessarily eliminate a small or temporary good accompanied or followed by
something proportionately worse. The Gadarene demoniac was indeed made stronger
by demonic possession, but his will was overridden in the process.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (6) <u>Divine miracle
is purposeful</u>. It is never for display of the supernatural (paranormal)
for its own sakelike magic shows. The cursing of the fig tree evidently had a
didactic significance (Matthew<b> 21</b>:18-22 = Mark <b>11</b>:12-14, 20-24);
the walking on the water was to get to the disciples and to save them from
destruction by the sea (Matthew <b>14</b>:22-23; Mark <b>6</b>:45-51; John <b>6</b>:15-21).
The floating ax head incident in 2 Kings <b>6</b>:1-7 is perhaps the least
purposive-looking of biblical miracles. In 1 Corinthians <b>14</b> Paul seems
to be drawing on the purposeful nature of true miracle when he calls for the
use of the <u>edification test</u> (<b>14</b>:26). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> During his
ministry Jesus did not reinforce interest in miracles as such: Luke <b>23</b>:8-12.
Jesus condemned the craving after signs: Matthew<b> 12</b>:38; <b>16</b>:1 (=
Mark <b>8</b>:11-12; Luke <b>11</b>:16); John <b>2</b>:18;<b> 6</b>:30.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (7) <u>Divine miracle
always succeeds</u>: Deuteronomy <b>18</b>:20-22; Acts </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>19</b>:13-17.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a) There
was a <u>two-stage</u> miracle when Jesus healed the blind </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> man:
Mark <b>8</b>:22-26.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) There
were two <u>failures</u> by the disciples during Jesus ministry: Peters
attempt to walk on the water (Matthew<b> 14</b>:28-31) and the disciples
failure to help the lunatic boy at the foot of the Mount of Transfiguration
(Matthew <b>17</b>:14-20 = Mark<b> 9</b>:14-29). Both failures were due to a
lack of faith on the part of the miracle worker; the second involved a lack of
prayer (and fasting?).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Regarding
uniform success in attempted miracle, we must keep in mind the desire of the
person in need of the benefit. This problem might apply to cases of demon
possession. Another implication is that one who is really doing supernatural
signs like miraculous healing or exorcisms has the guidance from God to know
whether a specific miracle should be attempted. If God does not want a person
healed, and if miracle always succeeds, then a person who does miracles for God
would have to know God did not want a one attempted; otherwise, uniform results
would not characterize miracle <i>vs</i>. answered prayer.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Since exorcism
is not called a spiritual gift in the New Testament, is it possible that
attempting to heal the lunatic boy resulted in failure because it should be
conceived of on the analogy of answered prayer rather than miracle? Is it possible
that a demoniac might not want to be cleansed from demonic possession and
that this was the case with the young boy?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (8)  <u>Divine miracle
is not done by immoral persons</u>: Mark <b>9</b>:39; John </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>9</b>:31, 33</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (a)  Matthew<b>
7</b>:22-23 is not an exception here. Though the passage </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> could
be interpreted to mean:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [1] Gods
speaking through an evil person who used his name. Considerations noted above
make this approach unacceptable, so another possibility must be sought, such as</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [2] Gods
speaking through an office held by an evil man (cp. one view of John <b>11</b>:47-53).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [3] A
formerly faithful person with gifts who subsequently apostatized (cp. Hebrews <b>6</b>:4-6);
yet note the word <i>never</i>. One who has fallen away is as good as never
saved at all.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [4] Someone
lacking love while possessing gifts (cp. 1 Corinthians <b>13</b>:1-
3). Again, we must ask, Does God endow someone who is not saved? Though God
might endow someone who misuses the gift, the lost condition in the judgment
would mean an apostasy situation again. There is the <u>love test</u>, but it
seems not to help the understanding of this passage. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [5] Someone
lying at the judgment (?)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [6] Someone
living within the general will of God, but not within the special will of God
for that person, which in effect is a refusal of God commandments </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  [7] People
who thought they were doing miracles by Gods hand, but in fact were doing them
by demonic power</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>   *[8] People
who thought they were doing miracles</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (b) The <u>moral
test</u> does not mean moral perfection. Paul was willing to admit his lack of
perfection (Philippians<b> 3</b>:12-16; 1 Timothy<b> 1</b>:15). Peter stood
condemned at his dissimulation in Antioch (Galatians <b>2</b>:11). Samson was perhaps
the most immoral of Gods chosen men.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  e. Divine miracle as
distinguished from paranormal phenomena</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>
