<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  To some people there is
no distinction between demonic and paranormal, but for our purposes we separate
them. The same tests will weed out paranormal phenomena. Examples of
paranormal phenomena include the following:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) Telekinesis: the
movement of objects at a distance by unaided will </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> power</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) Precognition: foreseeing
the future</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) Telepathy: the
apparent transfer of thought from one person to </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> another without
means</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) Apports: the
transference of objects from place to place even through </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> material
barriers</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5) Materialization: the
coming into real presence of persons or objects </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (6) Levitation: defiance
of the gravitation field</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (7) Clairvoyance: the
ability to see something mentally</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (8) Clairaudience: the
ability to hear something mentally</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (9) Extra-bodily
experiences: separation of the perceiving consciousness </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> from ones
bodily form</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  f.  Divine miracle as distinguished
from psychosomatic healing</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Divine miracle may call
for faith by the requester, but it does not depend </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> on it:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) Jesus required
faith in Matthew<b> 8</b>:13 (= Mark <b>5</b>:36); <b>9</b>:2 (Mark<b> 2</b>:5);
<b>9</b>:22 (Mark <b>5</b>:34; <b>10</b>:52; Luke <b>7</b>:50; <b>8</b>:48; <b>17</b>:19;
<b>18</b>:42; <i>your faith has made you whole</i>); <b>9</b>:28-29; 1<b>2</b>:58
(= Mark <b>6</b>:5); Mark <b>9</b>:23-24; <b>11</b>:23-24 (cp. Matthew <b>17</b>:20;
<b>21</b>:21; 1 Corinthians <b>13</b>:2); Luke<b> 8</b>:50;
cp. Acts <b>14</b>:9.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2)  The miracle did
not depend on faith in the case of Malchus in the Garden of Gethsemane (John <b>18</b>:10-
11) or the lame man at Gate Beautiful (Acts<b> 3</b>:1-10).  </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  g.  Summary of types of
miracles and their varying significances</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1)  The value of <u>nature</u>
miracles is that the result could not involve the psychosomatic element. That
question is often raised in connection with healing miracles.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2)  The value of <u>healing</u>
miracles is that they were signs with positive value, a doubtful thing to be
associated with satanic supernatural power.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3)  The value of <u>predictive</u>
miracles is that they cannot involve magic or </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> delusion. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  4. Reasons the Jews rejected Jesus
of Nazareth as Messiah </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a.  Jesus came from the wrong
place.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) As far as most
people knew Jesus came from Nazareth of Galilee, not from Bethlehem Judah as
predicted (John <b>7</b>:41-43, 52). The Jews understood that Messiah was to be
born in Bethlehem as predicted in Micah <b>5</b>:2 (Matthew<b> 2</b>:1-6; John <b>7</b>:41-43).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Their retort to
Nicodemus in John <b>7</b>:52 was that no prophet arises from Galilee, meaning
evidently that no <u>prediction</u> anticipated a prophet from there, because
Jonah did come from Gath-hepher in Zebulun (2 Kings <b>14</b>:25; Joshua <b>19</b>:13).
The comment still bears a curious ring because Isaiah <b>9</b>:1-2, cited in
Matthew <b>4</b>:15-16, has Messianic import (cp. <b>9</b>:3-7).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A related
observation comes into the discussion with the man born blind. The Pharisees
say that they do not know where Jesus came from (John<b> 9</b>:29), evidently
meaning something that would naturally contrast with <i>We know that God spoke
to Moses.</i> We would suppose that they knew where Jesus was from in a <u>geographical
sense</u>Galilee (John <b>7</b>:52). Presumably they could have discovered
from census records and perhaps from local commoners around Bethlehem that he
had been <u>born</u> in Judah. In Johns gospel, where he is from appears to
have a <u>spiritual sense</u>, meaning who sent him. In an earlier discussion
recorded in John <b>8</b>:12-20, 42, he claims to know where he came from and
where he was going, but the Pharisees did not (<b>8</b>:14). Similar comments
occur elsewhere in John <b>13</b>:3; <b>16</b>:28, 30.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) Interestingly a
second view of the Messianic origin expected him to appear suddenly in
Jerusalem, possibly on the strength of Daniel<b> 7</b>:13 or Malachi <b>3</b>:1.
Knowing where Jesus came from meant he was not the Messiah: John <b>7</b>:27;
cp. <b>6</b>:42.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  In reference to
the first view, the gospel records of Matthew <b>2</b>:1ff. and Luke <b>2</b>:1-7
indicate that he was indeed born in Bethlehem Judah before his parents took him
to Galilee at an early age. As for the second view, it may be said that Jesus
came ultimately from the Father and immediately through Bethlehem and Nazareth.
Perhaps the two origins could also correspond to his first and second comings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> b.  Jesus spoke of his death.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  According to John <b>12</b>:34,
the people understood that <i>Messiah abides forever</i> (Psalm <b>110</b>:4;
Isaiah <b>9</b>:7; Daniel <b>7</b>:14; <i>e.g</i>.) instead of dying as Jesus
predicted: John<b> 3</b>:14; <b>8</b>:28; Matthew <b>16</b>:21-28 (= Mark<b> 8</b>:31-9:1;
Luke<b> 9</b>:22-27); <b>17</b>:22-23 (= Mark <b>9</b>:30-32; Luke<b> 9</b>:43b-45);
<b>26</b>:1-5 (= Mark <b>14</b>:1-2; Luke <b>22</b>:1-2).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c.  Jesus
refused to abide by the traditions of the elders and by irrelevant </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-81.0pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> cultural
taboos.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) He frequently set
aside their Sabbath regulations: Matthew <b>12</b>:1-4 (= Mark <b>2</b>:23-<b>3</b>:6;
Luke <b>6</b>:1-11); Luke <b>13</b>:10-17; John <b>5</b>:9-18; <b>7</b>:19-24; <b>9</b>:1-41.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) He often did not
observe their ceremonial purifications: Matthew <b>15</b>:1-20 (= Mark <b>7</b>:1-23);
Luke <b>11</b>:37-41.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) He did not
practice apartheid with Gentiles, Samaritans, the multitudes, women,
foreigners, or publicans (sinners, people of the land, the <i>hoi polloi</i>):
Matthew <b>9</b>:10-13 (= Mark <b>2</b>:15-17; Luke<b> 5</b>:29-32); <b>11</b>:16-19
(= Luke<b> 7</b>:29-33); Luke<b> 4</b>:16-30; <b>7</b>:29-33; <b>15</b>:1-2ff; <b>19</b>:1-10;
John <b>4</b>:9-29.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) He did not
observe patterns of regularized fasting as did Johns and the Pharisees disciples:
Matthew<b> 9</b>:14-17 (= Mark <b>2</b>:18-22; Luke <b>5</b>:33-39); cp. Luke <b>18</b>:12.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5) Jesus criticized
the tradition of the elders as such: Matthew <b>15</b>:1-20 (= Mark <b>7</b>:1-23);
<b>23</b> on various points.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The Jews
identified the tradition of the elders with the intent of Mosaic legislation. A
breach of these regulations was regarded as a failure to understand or obey the
laws intent; that is, from a civil and ceremonial standpoint Jesus was unwilling
to submit to case law, which by precedent had interpreted the theocratic
regulations, or statutory law.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Jesus did tell
the multitudes to observe what the scribes and Pharisees enjoined because they
sat in Moses seat. As the Sanhedrin, or supreme court, had civil authority to
lead the nation, Jesus did not try to turn the populace against that authority.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>Since however, the authority was misused, he opposed
the misuse with his life, willing</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
color:red'> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'>to endure the consequences. There
is a loose comparison with Socrates and Athenian law. The misuse of authority
in case law sprang from a misunderstanding of divine intent in statutory law as
shown by the fact that the tradition of the elders contradicted the Mosaic Law
at various points despite the avowed intent to build a hedge around it. Matthew
<b>23</b> records an indictment of the quantifying mentality (23-24) that goes
to seed in a legal mindset on regulatory provisions. Law cannot easily deal
with anything but overt behavior (25-26), since inner motivation is difficult
to measure; yet mortals who cannot know the heart adequately must render
judgment. So time and energy concentrate on externals and neglect weightier,
internal matters.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d. Jesus said things that the
religious leaders took to be blasphemy.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Despite Isaiah<b> 9</b>:7
the religious leaders did not associate deity with the promised Messiah;
consequently, there was no way Jesus could come in that role and nature without
being refused by them on the grounds of blasphemy.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1) He claimed the
right to forgive sins: Matthew<b> 9</b>:1-8 (= Mark <b>2</b>:1-12; </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Luke <b>5</b>:17-26);
Luke<b> 7</b>:47-49.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (2) He claimed to be
the Son of God: Matthew<b> 26</b>:63-65 (Mark<b> 14</b>:61-</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> 64); John <b>10</b>:30-39;<b>
19</b>:7.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  By referring to
himself as son of God in reference to himself or calling God Father, Jesus
in their mind was making himself <i>equal with God</i> (John <b>5</b>:18). The
objection lies alongside their attempt to avoid impious use of Gods personal
name: they declared it ineffable. So ingrained was this tradition and for so
long honored that the pre-Christian Septuagint (LXX) translated the
tetragrammaton (</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt'></span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt'><span dir=LTR></span>, <i>Yahveh</i>)
with </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>, Lord). Gods personal Yahveh never appears in the
New Testament, perhaps in deference to these Jewish scruples so as not to cause
unnecessary problems, especially since <i>father</i> was the term Jesus taught
his disciple to use in referring to God. Jesus was not just dealing with his
hearers objection to seemingly unwise terminology; he was dealing with the
Jewish mentality of overkill seen in the refusal to use Gods name at all.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) He spoke against
the law: Matthew <b>5</b>:17; Luke <b>16</b>:17 (cp. Acts <b>6</b>:13-</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>14</b>;
21:28).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) He spoke against
the temple: Matthew <b>26</b>:60-61, 65 (Mark <b>14</b>:57-58, 64; cp. Acts <b>6</b>:13-14;
<b>21</b>:28); John <b>2</b>:19-20 (cp. Matthew <b>27</b>:40; Mark <b>15</b>:29).
His <u>destroying</u> the temple as the Pharisees charged may be somewhat
overstated at the trials of Jesus and Stephen, because the statement in John<b>
2</b>:19 is an imperative with a seemingly concessive implication: <i>If a
person destroyed the temple . . .</i> The rebuilding without hands (Mark <b>14</b>:58)
may have implied divine activity and so was blasphemous to his hearers. (See
Old Testament antecedents in Daniel <b>2</b>:34, 45; <b>8</b>:25; cp. Job <b>34</b>:20
and Zechariah <b>4</b>:6; for New Testament usages see Acts<b> 7</b>:48; <b>17</b>:24;
2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:1; Ephesians <b>2</b>:11; Colossians <b>2</b>:11;
Hebrews <b>9</b>:11, 24; cp.<b> 11</b>:10.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Having
misunderstood the nature and role of Messiah, the Jewish leaders could hardly
do other than condemn him for blasphemy because he claimed things that to them
were humanly impossible.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> e. Jesus did not
unequivocally identify himself as Messiah in front of the masses: Mark <b>1</b>:34;
Luke <b>4</b>:41; John <b>10</b>:24. Indeed he could not without the dangers of
misunderstanding associated with that claim.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  f.  Jesus did not lead out
in military fashion against foreign oppression of </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Israel: John <b>6</b>:15.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  g.  Some of his teaching made
no sense to them.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) Coming down out
of heaven: John<b> 6</b>:38, 58</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) Giving them his
flesh to eat and his blood to drink: John<b> 6</b>:51-59</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3)  Going where his
hearers could not come: John<b> 7</b>:33-36; <b>8</b>:21-23</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (4) Claiming
sinlessness: John<b> 8</b>:46-48</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (5) Promising that
the one who kept Jesus word would never die: John </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>8</b>:51-53;<b>
11</b>:25-26</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (6) Abrahams seeing
Jesus day: John <b>8</b>:56-59</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>At one point anyway, the crowds thought he was crazy
or had a demon, because he said they were trying to kill him (John <b>7</b>:19).
The people who spoke that way, however, may not have realized the speaker was
Jesus (cp.<b> 7</b>:25-26).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> h. Jesus posed a threat to
the authority of the religious leaders: John<b> 2</b>:13-22; </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Matthew <b>21</b>:12-16
(= Mark <b>11</b>:15-18; Luke <b>19</b>:45-47)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> i. He criticized the
hypocrisy of Israels leaders: Matthew <b>23</b>:1-39 (= Mark</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <b>12</b>:38 = 40; Luke <b>20</b>:45-47).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  j.  At least certain sectors
of the Jewish leadership (Sadducees?) feared that his popularity could
eventuate in the Roman destruction of the Jewish state altogether: John <b>11</b>:47-53.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Jesus had done
outstanding miracles that the leaders could not denynotably the resurrection
of Lazarus (John <b>12</b>:9-11), but he had shown no interest in military pursuits,
nor had he given them a sign from heaven like calling down fire to destroy
his enemies (Luke <b>9</b>:51-56).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Most of the Jews
rejecting Jesus came from their attempt to apply the <u>doctrinal test</u> and
the <u>moral test</u> of miracle as commanded in Deuteronomy <b>13</b>:1-5. That
the Pharisees were trying to override miracle with the doctrinal test is
evident from John <b>9:</b>16 (24) on the occasion of Jesus healing the man
born blind: <i>This man is not from God; he does not keep Sabbath,</i> a
comment that did not deny the miracle had occurred (24).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  They could not deny the
miracles, because some of them were astounding: the resurrection of Lazarus,
the healing of the man born blind, and so on. (cp. Acts<b> 4</b>:16). The
extreme number of signs Jesus performed made denial obscurantist. Their options
were either (a) to claim it was <u>demonic miracle</u> or (b) to claim that it
was <u>divine miracle</u> without authenticating the miracle worker. At least
three problems challenge the way the Pharisees applied the doctrinal test.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (1) Failure to
distinguish divine and demonic miracle</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  On two
occasions or more, the Pharisees assigned Jesus miracles to Satans power: (a)
the exorcising of a demon that caused dumbness (Matthew <b>9</b>:32-34; <b>10</b>:25);
(b) the exorcising of a demon causing blindness and dumbness (Matthew <b>12</b>:22-37
= Mark<b> 3</b>:19-30; Luke <b>11</b>:14-26; cp. Mark <b>3</b>:21). The calumny
came under the charge that he did his miracles by the power of Beelzebub (=
Baalzebub of 2 Kings <b>1</b>:2, 3, 6, 16). The clearest attempts to credit his
signs to demonic power came with these exorcisms (cp. Matthew 11:18; John<b> 7</b>:20;<b>
8</b>:48-52;<b> 10</b>:20).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Jesus raised
two objections: (a) Satan would be working against himself if he cast out his
own minions (Matthew<b> 12</b>:25-26; <i>etc</i>.); (b) As an <i><u>ad hominem</u></i>
argument, Jesus raised a question about the Pharisees claims to perform
exorcisms. In short, demonic miracle would not be directed against Satans
destructive purposes in the world.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The persistent
call for a sign from heaven in the face of Jesus constant miracles may have
been a request for a <u>kind</u> of miracle that would not only be stupendous,
but clearly not demonic (Matthew <b>12</b>:22-24 + 38; other examples are
Matthew <b>16</b>:1-4 = Mark<b> 8</b>:11-13; Luke<b> 11</b>:16; cp. John<b> 6</b>:30-32
as well as John<b> 2</b>:18 and 1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:22). The Old Testament
background for that request seems to be events like Moses and the manna (John <b>6</b>:30-31;
cp. Exodus <b>16</b>), Joels prophecy in <b>2</b>:28-32, or Elijah on Mount
Carmel (2 Kings <b>1</b>:10-12; cp. Luke<b> 9</b>:51-56). It may have been in
response to that desire for obviously supernatural divine miracle that the
disciples asked Jesus whether they should call down fire from heaven to destroy
the Samaritans who had not welcomed Jesus (Luke <b>9</b>:51-56).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (2) Failure to equate
divine empowerment with authentication of claims</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Upon healing
the man born blind, some Pharisees claimed that Jesus was a sinner because he
did not keep the Sabbatha legal and doctrinal test in combination (John <b>9</b>:16,
24). For this to make sense, we have to assume that God might give his power to
someone who was not doctrinally and morally sound. Other Pharisees doubted that
a sinner could do such signs (John <b>9</b>:16). The blind man himself argued
that God does not enable those who oppose him because signs authenticate
claims.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  What the
Pharisees had in mind was a case like that of Samson, whose moral level was
low; yet he demonstrated supernatural strength. A similar case might be the
possibility of misusing gifts as the Corinthians did. While such a thing might
be possible, the Samson example shows that Gods Spirit at least eventually
leaves a person that is a sinner and grieves the Spirit of God beyond some
limit. In keeping with that point, Jesus from the beginning referred to the
long haul that would end in his resurrection and the establishment of the church
(John <b>2</b>:18-22; Matthew <b>12</b>:38-45 = Luke <b>11</b>:29-32; Matthew <b>16</b>:4).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-112.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  (3) Failure
to consider the possibility that they misunderstood the Old </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Testament</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Jesus told the
Pharisees they were blind because they said, <i>We see</i> (John <b>9</b>:40-41).
Their behavior indicated an unwillingness to be instructed on such matters
(John <b>9</b>:34; <b>8</b>:37, 43; cp. Matthew<b> 13</b>:13-15). The essential
problem was pride.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Applying the
doctrinal test assumes a proper understanding of the doctrine. The religious
leaders of Jesus day had for so long understood the statutory law in the way it
was interpreted by the case law (traditions of the elders) that they had lost
the distinction between the two. Having lost the distinction, they accused
Jesus of heresy when he broke, not the spirit of the statutory law, but the
letter of case law.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> k. Elijah had not yet come
(Malachi <b>3</b>:1-2).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:67.5pt'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Several items associated with Elijah come into
the gospel accounts. Jesus said of John the Baptist, <i>This is Elijah who was
to come</i> (Matthew <b>11</b>:12-19; (cp. Luke <b>7</b>:18-25). Herod was
afraid that John had resurrected (Mark <b>6</b>:14-16). Peter responded to
Jesus question about who people were saying he was by saying, <i>Some say
Elijah . . . </i>(Matthew <b>16</b>:13-20; Mark<b> 8</b>). On the Mount of
Transfiguration, Elijah appeared along with Moses. Peter may have thought that
this was the fulfillment of the prediction about Elijahs coming (Matthew <b>17</b>:1-13;
Mark). Finally, Jesus cry of dereliction on the cross was misheard by some of
the bystanders as a call for Elijah (Matthew <b>27</b>:45-50). As far as we can
tell, the prophecy meant that someone would come in the spirit and power of
Elijah, as it is often put, rather than Elijah himself. Another option would
be to assign Elijahs return to yet future events associated with Christs
second coming.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  C.  Consequence</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1.  Individual</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The triangular schematic of
(1) need, (2) basis, and (3) result pictures <u>apologetics</u> as starting
with (1) need. <u>Bibliology</u> (canonicity) is the same set, starting with
(2) basis. <u>Evangelism</u> is the same set again but starting at (3) result
because the church evangelizes into the need of the world around it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  In its largest dimension we
must see Christianity as an interpersonal community rather than at the
individual level. Love becomes its characteristic feature in practical effect. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  If the church is saved people
serving in divine pattern, then love is the moving force in that service. Since
the church is the aggregate of individuals, each person bears the mark of a loving
that gives rise to loving action. <u>Love addresses the point of contact in
reaching a world in need</u>. Love creates trust, an object for mans sense of
dependence. It produces a sense of meaning existentially in community and
eschatologically in. The point of contact in people is their ability to be
loved.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <u>Love provides the
mechanisms for conversion</u>. First, love takes the initiative; so reversing
the downward spiral of deteriorating relationships comes by an act of
unprovoked love. Secondly, love is synoptic, functional, interpersonal, and
reciprocal. It addresses interpersonal relationships and touches on everything
that comprises persons in community. Love is both rational and emotional, so it
combines cognitive and affective with the social dimension through reciprocal
behavior. By making love central in the real world of history, Christianity
affords a higher self in its relations. Thirdly, love is resistible, so it
avoids determinism while fostering positive response. In conversion influential
love replaces supernatural miracle and stimulus-response. Love comes close to
being self-authenticating experience.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <u>Love provides the mechanism
for sanctification</u>. Love motivates, brings self-control, and admits of
degrees; so it gives the strength to overcome sin by generating motivational
power to conquer habit. With salvation comes the sense of acceptance into
fellowship with the Spirit of God and the increased potential for development
over pre-Christian possibilities.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2. Church</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Love integrates the Christian
faith, because Christianity deals with the art of living relationally.
Ultimately the only promise in the faith is a better quality of interpersonal
relations. Logic provides the bond between ideas; physical laws provide the
bonds in nature; love provides the bond between persons; authority connects the
steps in legal process. Natural depravity is impersonal, which shows up in
misformulated emphasis on divine holiness instead of a balance of holiness and
love. In its place, we see a construct that is resistible so as to avoid
determinism and yet triumphant over non-Christian systems. It must admit of
degrees to be better than the pre-Christian condition and yet not productive of
sinlessness. That construct is holy love.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-right:-.5in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> II.  Correlation with the Principles of
Canonicity</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  A.  General schematic</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  In keeping with the structure of
apologetics, the following sketch, summary, and explanation deal with the
principles for determining what materials should be present in the
Judaeo-Christian canon.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> <img width=407 height=169
src="/cir-library/images/essays/part-i-overview-of-christian-apologetics/image006.png"></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  <u>Principle</u>
 <u>Function</u>  <u>Locus</u> <u>Passage</u></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> OBJECTIVE  A. the historical  positive
 origin of 2 Peter <b>1</b>:16-21</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  principle  the
book</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> OBJECTIVE  B. the doctrinal  restrictive
 content of  Mark <b>2</b>:9-12</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> principle  the
book</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> SUBJECTIVE C. the subjective  confirmatory
 impact on  1 Corinthians </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> principle 
 the reader  <b>14</b>:24-25</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1.  The historical principle</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Whereas apologetics starts in
the present subjective, the principles of canonicity begin in the objective
past, canon being the body of literature that stands as authoritative,
definitive, and normative.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The historical principle is
logically first because canonicity has first to do with (5) <u>where</u> the
writings came from, which means who wrote them and where authors got their
information. Behind this step lies the more basic concern of (4) <u>how</u> a
person was recognized as one with a divine message vouchsafed to him and
distinguished from false claimants. The question is answered by observing that Gods
inbreaking into the world in special revelation always involves (3) <u>miracle</u>.
Even (for that matter) the prophet must be able to distinguish between (2) <u>divine</u>
miracle as an authenticating occurrence and demonic miracle, parapsychological
phenomena, deception, and the like. That distinction is most decisively made by
(1) the <u>triumph of the divine</u> in confrontation: the resurrection of
Israel from Egypt and the resurrection of Messiah from the grave are the
apologetic and theological starting points for the Old and New Testament canons
respectively. These are historical considerations, because revelation is a historical
matter, rather than a metaphysical construct. The five points inside the
historical principle are the same five points in the authentication of a
message. That correlation occurs because canonicity has to do with the written
form of the same thing a prophet proclaims. The historical principle determines
canonicity by identifying the right books in the first place; it is positive.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.  The doctrinal principle</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The doctrinal principle
determines canonicity by eliminating further books that do not harmonize with
previous revelation miraculously authenticated in confrontation. Any writing
that contains material contradictory to previous revelation is false and
non-canonical, not only in the matter at issue, but as a whole. It is without
status among the people of God. The doctrinal principle serves to eliminate new
books from falsely coming into the canon; it is restrictive.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3.  The subjective principle</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The subjective principle
confirms in personal experience the objective claims and promises of
revelation. That sense of truthfulness means having the ring of truth. It
speaks to mans need and finds acceptance there. It is the reality referred to
under the expression the inner witness of the Spirit (Romans <b>8</b>:14-16;
John <b>19</b>:4-5, 16). In the present view, this sense of trustworthiness is
not a direct act on a man by the Spirit parallel to the proclamation, but as a
natural experience a person may have in matters not distinctively religious or
Christian. People intuitively perceive the correlation between the answer and
their need. Intuitively means it is not something reasoned to. Correlation
is like seeing a bolt and a nut and perceiving that they go together. In the
case of an unbeliever, it is the intuitive recognition of the correlation
between answer received and need felt. In the case of a believer, it is the
intuitive recognition of the correlation between present claims and previous
revelation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Other principles set aside in
this treatment have been proposed by writers from a range of theological
persuasions: antiquity of the writing, written in a certain language, and the
like. The three above, however, are deemed sufficient for explaining the
theoretical mechanics of canonization.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The present set of the
determining principles differs from some other approaches to canonicity in the
number and order of basic principles. It is common to put the doctrinal
principle in primary position. Luther is frequently cited in his dictum that
those books are authoritative which preach Christ, although he may have meant
it as a hermeneutical guide rather than a canonical principle. At any rate,
three weaknesses inhere in such a suggestion. (a) The doctrinal principle can
only be restrictive in that it can only invalidate false doctrines that
contradict previous revelation. It <u>cannot deal with false teaching that does
not contradict previous revelation</u>. (b) The doctrinal principle <u>cannot
determine the original body of literature</u> in terms of which the doctrinal
criteria are first established without becoming circular in reasoning. The
problem, for example, is insoluble as to why what preaches Christ should have
any authority. (c) Primacy of the doctrinal principle <u>tends to create a
canon within a canon</u>. There is not much preaching about Christ in Job,
Haggai, Esther, or the Song of Solomon. By the principle chosen such books must
fall into a secondary position of authority, whose contents are negotiable.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Another common tendency,
especially among Reformed theologians, is to advance the subjective principle
to first position. Because of human depravity, the <u>unbeliever</u> cannot
recognize divine truth until a secret, regenerative operation of the Spirit
occurs parallel to hearing the message. It stands as an avowed mystery how God
implants the conviction of canonicity in the heart of the <u>elect</u>. This
kind of subjectivism addresses little that is definitive in canonicity or
apologetics, because at best these related matters are <i>post facto</i> as far
as becoming a Christian is concerned.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  4.  Practical providential matters</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  In the three previous
principles, an equation was made between what was written by an inspired man
and what was canonical, because canonicity was being considered as to <u>origination</u>.
Canonization may also be considered as to <u>formalization</u>. We know from
scripture itself, however, that not all original material has been included in
the Judaeo-Christian canon. From the Old Testament period there are several
cases in point:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  a. The Book of the Wars of
the Lord (Numbers <b>21</b>:14)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  b. The Book of Jashar
(Joshua <b>10</b>:13; 2 Samuel <b>1</b>:18)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  c. The History of Samuel the
Seer (1 Chronicles <b>29</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  d. The History of Nathan the
Prophet (1 Chronicles <b>29</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  e. The History of Gad the
Seer (1 Chronicles <b>29</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  f. The History of Shemaiah
the Prophet (2 Chronicles <b>12</b>:15)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  g. The History of Iddo the
Seer (2 Chronicles <b>12</b>:15)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  h. The Visions of Iddo the
Seer (2 Chronicles <b>9</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  i. The Commentary of Iddo
the Prophet (2 Chronicles <b>13</b>:22)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  j. The Vision of Isaiah the
Prophet, the Son of Amoz (2 Chronicles <b>32</b>:22 = </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Isaiah?)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  k. The Prophecy of Ahijah
the Shilonite (2 Chronicles <b>9</b>:29)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.25in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  l. The
Book/History of the Acts/Chronicles of (Various Kings of Israel and </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:-1.25in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> Judah)
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> m. Elijah the prophet wrote
something (2 Chronicles<b> 21</b>:12).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  From the New Testament the
Epistle to the Laodiceans (Colossians <b>4</b>:17) comes to mind, although it
may be the same as Ephesians. An earlier letter to Corinth is another
possibility (1 Corinthians <b>5</b>:9). About all that can be done in these
instances is to suggest such as the following:</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (1)  They may not have
had lasting or universal significance. Several Old Testament items could have
been destroyed during the Babylonian capture of Jerusalem.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (2)  They may not have
had public significance such as for lectionary use. Note the textual problems
of Romans <b>15-16</b>. Philemon, 2 and 3 John were retained, however, and the
Laodicean letter was evidently useful for public assembly.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (3)  The intention of
the writer may be decisive in some New Testament cases if Paul, for example,
oversaw the collection of his own letters. New Testament books were all written
by an apostle or one who served as an extension of his office.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> (4)  Perhaps some
books could be done without, but others that could be kept or lost God might
providentially let go either way.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'> At any rate, the
formalization of the canon operated in terms of principles operative in the
origination of the canon, so those principles of the former dictate the results
of the latter. As in most cases involving multiple principles, one of these is
decisive while the others play important secondary roles. The decision on
canonicity is by now a matter of record since we no longer have direct, or even
indirect, access to the evidence taken into consideration originally. Nevertheless,
as a discipline, understanding the principles has important implications for
hermeneutics, for claims of further revelation during the church age, and for
evaluating lost books that might come to light. These principles combine to
establish what books are canonical, that is, which ones have normative
authority for the faith. Canonicity correlates with God-breathedness (2 Timothy
<b>3</b>:16) and equals the concept of apostolicity. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  B.  Sufficiency of written testimony</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  1.  <i>Vs</i>. personal observation</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  It is unrealistic to demand
personal observation as the only basis for belief. Most of what we come to know
is through the written or oral testimony of other people (2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:7).
Jesus pronounced a blessing on those who have not seen and yet believe (John<b>
20</b>:29), presumably because only sufficient evidence is needed to convince
someone whose attitude is open to truth that questions self-centeredness.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  2.  <i>Vs</i>. oral tradition</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Written testimony possesses
greater stability than does oral tradition. After a generations time the
reliability of oral information significantly declines. It is eyewitness
testimony that the biblical materials purport to preserve in written form.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  3.  <i>Vs</i>. modern miracle</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  Generally speaking, miracle
authenticates new revelation more than re-authenticating old revelation for
each new generation. Elijah on Mount Carmel shows, of course, that continued
miracle may occur; but in a significant sense most prophetic miracle did not so
much re-authenticate the Mosaic covenant as it did authenticate <u>guidance-revelation</u>
delivered through the prophets of Israel or <u>new revelation</u> progressively
made known. Christianity has generally taught that progressive revelation did
not continue beyond the apostolic era with implication that miracle does not necessarily
have to continue. Once new revelation has been authenticated, the reliability
of a written record of that authentication is deemed sufficient.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>  The point here is in regard to
sufficiency for apologetic purposes. This does not disallow special guidance or
even continued revelation, especially in the form of enablement to understand. The
response of Abraham to the rich man in Jesus parable sets the expectancy: <i>They
have Moses and the prophets; let them hear them</i> (Luke <b>16</b>:29-31). It
was not necessary for someone to go back to the rich mans brothers and make them
believe.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.25in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:11.0pt'>christir.org</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-top:0in;margin-right:-.5in;margin-bottom:0in;
margin-left:.5in;margin-bottom:.0001pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt'></span></p>
</div>
